That need for new career experiences and new challenges comes at the cost of making me move onto new projects more often than others. That being said, I’m definitely a shipping kind of person. I know how to stay with a thing to get it out the door and beyond, but I definitely didn’t stick around for Gears 2, 3, 4, or 5. I needed new challenges and ventured out to find them. I have mad respect for some of my fellow artists and ADs who have stuck it out at companies, on projects, with franchises for decades, but my path just doesn’t flow that way.


Q. What about the role is the most enjoyable to you? The part that gets you out of bed and keeps you motivated, that keeps the brain fizzing and sparking with creative ideas?


A. 
Creating exciting experiences for players. I don’t actually care if it’s interactive, passive, live, recorded, I just want the thing I’m working on to have elements that are so unexpected you have to see it again. This is what drives me. 


Sure, I have to work on things that fall into a much more traditional box, but it’s the projects that push me and the team that get me out of bed. If it’s scary and we’re not sure we can pull it off, that’s the project for me. I love working on the cutting edge. It requires outside-the-box problem solving all the time. It requires that each asset, each choice, each macro and micro decision are a mix of experience and guessing you’ve got it right. Adjusting constantly to things not working right, to things breaking, to the team being stretched beyond their inherent knowledge base.


The cutting edge is not for the faint of heart. I’ve worked on multi-million-dollar activations that ended badly because things failed at the worst possible times. 


However, the rewards when you pull off something new is magical. It’s the kind of feeling you only get a handful of times in a long career. I’ve been lucky enough to work with companies and teams who trust me enough to travel that path with me, to trust me that I can lead a team to something truly great.


Q. What’s your least favourite part of the role? Anything common you’ve found from company to company, or does it depend on the environment? (There’s no judgement here!)


A. 
My least favourite part of the role is likely the management side of things. Having spent a bunch of years directing, in a more traditional sense of the word, the video games industry’s requirement that ADs are also managers is less than ideal.


Managing people and being able to lead a team of artists to make a cohesive visual statement are two completely different skills. The day-to-day role of art directing is communicating an artistic vision to a team and drive towards that vision across all the crafts. ADs work with design to understand the goals of the project so that the aesthetic choices complement and elevate the game’s design and playstyle. This means the AD’s job is to work with each of the visual crafts to create that cohesive visual wrapper for the core design and playstyle. From concept art to modelling to animation, each choice being made can either help or hurt the goal of design and art working together to make something truly amazing. 


Alongside design, the technology platform the game is on will also come with its own set of positive and negative traits that have to be navigated by art. The AD will need to understand how to lean into the positive traits and avoid the negative traits for the art to succeed. These can and will be different for each project because of what the goals and design are. Tech that works great for one thing doesn’t work as great for the next. A PBR doesn’t do you much good if you’re pushing for a cartoony/unrealistic style. 


Back to the question. All of that is to say that the job of art directing isn’t the same as art managing. Most companies conflate the two things because some ADs are actually managers with an art opinion versus artists with some management skills. I think the industry is the poorer for this mindset. I’ve seen some amazing artists who could and should direct a project fall to the wayside because they don’t have the manager/producer side of the role buttoned up.


Q. What do you think is required for a prospective Art Director and what contributes to their success?


A. 
The ability to communicate the language of visuals is everything. I’m not saying you have to have your colour theory down rote, but you need to understand what makes good artwork and what makes bad art not work. This isn’t about a gut feel, this is about real knowledge and experience in the art of visual design and storytelling. And this isn’t about style. This is about appeal.


Appeal isn’t about looking good or clean or sexy. It’s about the feeling you get when you look at it. That the flow of the shapes work, the colours work together; the animation fits the style; the lighting is ideal, etc. Appeal is something that an AD is dialling in from the first concept to the final frame. Every style has its appeal, or you wouldn’t have chosen it, and the AD’s job is to understand the nuances and make choices to keep the visuals driving on that chosen path.


I’m not saying the AD has to be the end-all knowledge keeper for style. Some ADs have folks on their teams who are more dialled in on a particular style and will lean on that person/s for help. This is collaboration, and there’s nothing wrong with that. That kind of art directing is just as valid, and the team will surely appreciate getting to be part of the decision and directing process. But one person will end up being held accountable for the shipped visuals, and that’s the AD. I have to always remember that, at the end of the day, just because someone is passionate about their opinion doesn’t make it right. 


The book Blink by Malcolm Gladwell was the first time I read something that describes a large portion of the job of the AD. To see something visual, have an opinion, then be able to articulate clearly the good and the bad. What works and what doesn’t. An AD’s Blink is built on experience and self-awareness. Understanding your own reactions and knowing how to hold on to them and use them to direct art and artists. Knowing when something gives you the tickle that means it’s risky but really cool, versus the tickle that it’s risky and probably really bad takes time to develop. That’s what some people call trusting their gut, but for me unless I can articulate why I think it will end well, I’m not doing my job correctly.


Q. Salary/package negotiation can still be a tricky area for some to handle, even after years in the industry. What advice would you give on getting a good deal? Any tips on technique? 


A. 
This is a tough area, for sure. I’ve built a career over many decades but that also came with lots of variations on salary versus bonus versus stock, etc. I’ve taken pay cuts to work at companies I believed would take off (Epic) and have forgone stocks for salary only to see the company get bought or their stocks go through the roof. 


I think fundamentally having an awareness of your own value is the starting point. Luckily most of the larger companies have well-researched pay scales they use now that reach across the video games industry and the VFX industry. 


In addition, an understanding of the role is key to what you ask for as well. If you’re being asked to be more of an Art Manager but that’s not where you love spending your energy, you need to be upfront about that and ask for compensation that makes that ok for you.


I’ve been a part of a number of start-ups and start-up–like companies, so I think I’m willing to risk a lot for the craft regardless of the pay. This is also something to keep in mind. Is the project worth taking a cut on, would you do it for less? As I mentioned, most companies have a pay scale, they’ll likely target someone based on experience and industry acclaim but that breaks a bit with folks who have done this longer. 


Being a freelancer is a great way to learn what people are willing to pay for your skills. I was a freelancer for seven years following leaving Epic and was able to really dial in what people were willing to pay for my knowledge and skills. 


Q. For you, what’s been the project that’s been the most memorable, and why? 


A. 
Gears of War
 is the project that I feel like encapsulated so much of what I love about art, video games, technology and filmmaking. 


A little backstory to help set the stage on why this project just hit all the right buttons for me: I was already a working video game artist when I went to film and art school. The industry was young and didn’t pay a whole lot so I figured film would be my real job. But lo-and-behold things took off and while I was directing films at school, I was starting a game studio with a friend.


The film school experience would play a big part in my approach to making art for games. Not just the direction of actors but the way lighting works in film, the way a set is built and dressed, etc. Each part of what goes into building a cinematic frame would eventually come to bear on how I pushed for visuals on projects I worked on.


As graphics technology advanced and we entered into the normal maps part of its history Epic Games went to work on the real-time engine that would redefine how games were made and how they could look. Lucky for me, I joined Epic during the early days of UE3’s creation. 


The game we were meant to make had started life as a sprawling vehicle combat experience but was rebooted as a third person actioner to help sell the visual abilities of the new Unreal Engine 3. So, 
Gears
 started in earnest with an engine being built from scratch and a killer team of artists. 


The game would be an action horror game which fit my sensibilities perfectly. The game would lean on cutting-edge technology and tools, and we would make heavy use of motion capture to help imbue the characters and the camera with a level of realism befitting the amazing graphics technology being built.


The unknowns were a constant and never scared me. The push for the graphics to be so amazing everyone would have to use Unreal Engine gave the art team a decidedly bold place to work from. The collaboration between gameplay and art was the best of any project I’ve ever worked on. Each was working to take what the other was doing and highlight it in the coolest way possible. A creature designed for a cinematic becomes a boss fight. The main characters having to be designed to be as badass as the bad guys. The cover gameplay, leading us to make them feel heavy and impactful in motion. Each step of the way through development it felt like every single person on the team was aligned around what we were doing. And it wasn’t a large team. Maybe 30ish people.


There was a point in production where I really could feel all the film-school knowledge coming to bear on the project; motion capturing the cameras both for in-game loops and for cinematics, to the colour correction tools that we had worked into the tools so that we could truly push a cinematic look, to the way we looked at lighting, VFX everything. We weren’t out to copy a look from a movie, we were out to make a game that felt like it was on par with any big-budget film being released. A turning point in tech and the art of games, IMO.


I tell people 
Gears
 was the first and last time I worked on a project that fit me so perfectly, fit my background and skills, and that hit at just the perfect time. A rare moment in a multi-decade career to have everything align in such a way that works out so amazingly. I feel lucky to have been a key part of that project and what it allowed me to do. BTW the dopamine hit from Gears being so successful lasted for years. 


Q. An AD can find themselves bogged down with more meetings than art. How do you recommend new ADs cope and are there any things they can do to carve out more art time?


A. 
Meetings are the bane of an artist’s existence. But they’re also a vital part of how a group of people communicate and align to keep moving in the same direction.


One of the things that I would expect any AD to do is being the leader of what kinds of meetings, how many, etc the art team are a part of. I have a tendency to challenge the usefulness of meetings on a constant basis within any team I’m on. If stand-ups turn into a long meeting with too many people that don’t need that level of visibility, then my job is to bring it up and alter it.  If a sync meeting has somehow morphed into a Favro update meeting with a producer driving, I question if this isn’t better done over Slack. 


Meeting times are also a key. Clumped on days and at times so that the AD and the team get large chunks of focused time for actual craft. For new ADs or vet ADs, the need to be that advocate for the team is vital. But it’s also vital that the AD has context across a lot more of the development process. A balancing act that requires thoughtful reflection on each ritual the team sets up, the cadence of the ritual and constantly being attentive to whether the meeting is of use. At the AD level, you’re expected to be vocal about how to best keep the artists and yourself working against the targeted visuals and deadlines. Don’t be shy to question the usefulness of a meeting and have that honest conversation with the producers and others. People’s individual styles also play a role in meeting culture, as some folks prefer meetings to slack communication. 


If an AD has tasks, they are on the hook for that and require focus time for actually creating, then the producers will respect that need and can help carve out the time, move meetings, etc to help accommodate. There isn’t an easy answer here. Meetings are an unfortunate by-product of working on a team. Communication and communication styles complicate this as well. As an AD it’s best to navigate this and check in with your artists along the way to make sure they feel they’re getting what they need from you and from the meetings. 


Q. Part of the job is to work with the exec team. How do you handle that conflict of the business personality versus the artistic personality?


A. 
This is heavily dependent on the company and the individuals involved. I’ve worked at companies where the business folks are super supportive and don’t require being managed and then at others where they had massive sway over a project down to the shape and look of a smoke trail.


Managing execs is partly the AD’s job, but I would also push a lot of that onto the exec producer on the team. I feel like their job is to manage non-art folks and my job is to make awesome looking art. Having a good dialogue and working relationship with the senior/exec producer is likely key to this being painful or not.   


Q. In today’s climate of greater personal care and corporate responsibility, where does the tricky subject of crunch lie? Can you foresee a future without death marches on large AAA titles?


A. 
I’ve never been a fan of crunch or death marches, but we all know they happen. I’m not sure for AAA games that have set release dates there will be much ability to get around this. Much like big movie releases, we want as much amazing as they can pack into it. 


Don’t think I have a good answer for this. However, I can tell you that a company can do a lot to mitigate this. For example, Riot has a pretty great anti-crunch stance that has played out well in the seven years I’ve been there. But 
League
 is a “constantly shipping game” so it’s shipping smaller bits over time versus a big release. 


Q. In the new normal, have you brought any new techniques into your life to make directing smoother – software, thinking, process?


A. 
I think tele-meetings are pretty effective in many ways. Much more effective than many people thought they were before the pandemic. Within that realm I think we’re all unhappy with the limitations of Hangouts, Slack, Zoom for art reviews and visual sharing. There are tools out there developed for film that are better, but they’re not as well known or used in game companies yet (I’m not talking about Shotgun, which I hate).


Overall, I think the pandemic has shown us how many of our meeting and syncs could be done remote and then some that really do require being in person. 


Q. When you look back over your career, what piece of advice would young Jerry have loved to have known ahead of time?


A. 
You’ll never have to go back to your job at Blockbuster because you’re going to build this hybrid career that bridges film, art, and video games. So, keep learning, keep making things, keep being excited by new technologies and being unafraid to learn them. You’ll have heartache and you’ll see projects crash and burn, but you’ll also win some amazing awards and have an impact on the industry that is felt around the world. People might not know your name when it’s all said and done, but you’ll have had a hell of a time and worked with some amazing folks along the way.


Q. The roadie run in GOW
 – was that all your idea? 


A. 
Crap, was it? I know I did that camera tests to pitch the idea (I still have that video). I wanted the camera to feel like a documentary filmmaker was trailing Marcus along his journey, so running behind him with a camera felt like the right thing to do. It made it feel chaotic and immediate and unlike anything we had seen before. But memory doesn’t tell me if it was my idea or a by-product of a larger idea. 


But camera was definitely something I had been keen to make feel more cinematic all through my career. To this day, the spline camera look I see in many games and game commercials drives me nuts. It’s not hard to make them feel more real. It’s just lack of knowledge or desire to do it.


Jerry O’Flaherty


https://www.imdb.com/name/nm1720331/
 


You’ll have noticed a familiar thread running here, everyone does things slightly differently. Depending on your background, education, confidence, desire for thrill or consistency and the type of project, they all factor into shaping your future.  It’s also interesting to note his thoughts on ADs having to be both artist and manager in our industry. He advocates greater use of an Art Manager, someone to take the burden of team management away so the AD can spend more time crafting the vision, which on reading now, makes more and more sense to me.


What type of Art Director will you be? 


Entering this new arena can feel a little intimidating, with all the artists looking to you to create the new vision of the project, or even take what has gone before on a previous project and advance it to the next level (a sequel). In my experience there are two types of projects and three types of Art Director and I’ll explain my thinking on this as we progress.


Two types of projects 


As I’ve already hinted, one of these is the sequel. For someone new to the Art Director role, it’s the perfect introduction. The style of the project is set, existing art pipelines are functional (though will need updating), and the team can start pumping out prototype art for the new game.


For a sequel to a successful title, it’s usually more about how to advance the artwork, using newer technology to improve the visuals or the world. Often publishers think bigger is better, so they, along with the project’s leaders, will set the scope accordingly for what you and your team are expected to fill.


As a new director, it will be down to you, the Design Director and Creative Director, to figure out the next new thing. Often concepts are design driven, and conversations tend to go something like: ‘Ok, it’s set in a snowy world, it’s free roaming, post-apocalyptic and this one, you and your teammate are travelling in an old renovated APC’. That’s how the snowball starts rolling. With the initial design direction, plus discussions about narrative and missions, weaponry, characters, enemies, the ball gets bigger and increasingly defined.


I expect you’ll be working with an established concept art team, and chances are they’ll know more about the project than you do! It’s a matter of finding how you all can contribute to the new game. Ultimately, however, you are the boss and there will probably be some ruffling of feathers and posturing as you establish your position in the team. That’s life, and video game life is competitive. As you gain knowledge and make good decisions, you’ll gradually gain their respect, and if your artwork is better than theirs, that’s even better and you’ll settle in faster.


The second type of project is the most challenging because of its complexity and scope. This is the brand-new IP, and it’s even more of an uphill push if it’s on a new generation console. Facing a blank canvas is exciting and daunting at the same time. This is a project for the experienced Art Director. It draws on all your experience to push and develop the project to hit not only your own artistic standards, but also those of your publishers and executive team.


Be prepared to make some hard calls, and to see your own and your team’s work made, remade, killed, brought back to life, rebuilt, shaders changed, scope increase, bloat, reduce, and then meet somewhere in the middle. Make no mistake, I’m really underselling the challenges faced on a new IP; the levels of skill and patience needed are Herculean.


There is an upside to this, and as we heard Jerry O’Flaherty in 
‘Art Director slice of life’
 advise, working on a game that breaks new ground and looks awesome is the holy grail of making games. It’s the endgame of being an AD, to be part of something truly special and know that you and your team have made that happen. If it goes well, then you will have contributed to a piece of entertainment that has touched millions of people.


But shipping the game is a long way from the start of a game. Throughout the life cycle of the project, it’s impossible not to change as a person; the challenges you and your team will face pull on all your existing skills and force you to grow and develop as the project progresses. At least, those are my thoughts on the process, but all Art Directors are not the same and not all grow in the same way. I suspect people don’t even consider that there might be a right or wrong way. I don’t think there is, as it’s an organic mix of you, your attitude, the company, the culture and the opportunities presented.


Personalities


Some directors are micro managers, some are control freaks, some are inspirational, some are pure artists, while we might even describe the rare exceptions as geniuses; you can bet your bottom dollar there’s one thing in common with all of them, and that is that they’ve all worked crazy hard to get to where they are. So far, I’ve worked with these four types of Art Director:


•
 
The heavy art hitter
 – Name any major game and there’s a good chance you can attribute it to one person’s vision. They will be a hugely experienced artist, offering strong art and design skills. Typically, this is a pure, driven artist, who can’t settle unless they are painting/building/creating.


•
 
The strong manager
 – This person hits deadlines, demonstrates strong organisational skills, knows exactly how to direct their concept team to devastating effect, and is part of the creative mix. They have clear vision and drive, and they use it to leverage the creative force that is the art team to help shape the gaming environment.


•
 
The film director mix
 – This is someone with advanced cinematography skills, including a big background in artwork and who understands how to use the concept team to their utmost creativity. The advantage of this style is being able to see scenes as if through a lens, wrapping all the skills used on a movie set or TV to bring a fresh perspective to the game world.


•
 
The combo
 – A mixture of any of the three categories depending on that person’s creative journey, often able to work well with managing the team and pushing along artwork, pipelines or cinematics, improving tech and art team culture side by side. 


Could there be more hiding out there in the world? Sure, there are! These are the ones I’ve seen in my time around the world, and you could be another completely new type. The games industry is constantly changing and developing. 


One thing is obvious, though. There is no one clear way, you define and make your own path. 


Big vision, big politics, big sell 


On becoming an Art Director (AD), you become a leader of art, a leader of communication and a diplomat (well, some do). These elements can be a little like mixing oil, water and sand, because it’s hard to combine the passion of putting your heart and soul into setting a vision with putting yourself out there to face all manner of opinions. 


I once worked for an Art Director who said, ‘If it’s been done already, I don’t want to see it!’ He wasn’t interested in copying other movies or games or other people’s styles. It was one of the best pieces of advice I could have been given, and it sank in deep.


But here’s the rub. Some companies are risk averse; well, most of them are to be honest, and the bigger they are, the slower they are to turn to new fresh thinking. This is often why you’ll see people leaving large stable set-ups to try more exciting and agile ventures, setting up their own companies. As an AD, you’re part of the creative team trying to persuade others to adopt this new way forward so that the company grows in new and exciting ways. It’s not quite that simple, though.


The reality, like it or not, is that you’re entering an arena of heavy politics and opinion. The difference is that you’re now closely linked with the business side of the gaming establishment, which you were aware of before, but now you face front and centre. If they have any art problems, they know who to go to, and it’s you. Departments such as legal, sales and marketing, or publishing, as well as senior directors, studio CEOs and other Art Directors all attempt to weigh in on the new vision, all trying to get their own agenda across. If someone says they don’t have an agenda, they’re not being honest, because we’re all driven by something. 


Understanding who the movers and shakers of the company are really helps, and because there are people within the gaming organisation who are particularly ruthless, it’s important to know who they are and who gets shown respect whether or not you like them. This may sound unsavoury, but it’s competitive out there, especially at the top. The fewer obstacles you must jump over, the better, when trying to get your ideas across at board level. The other side of the coin is that if you have the heavy hitters on your side, then your prospects of success are much higher.


If there’s one thing I’ve learnt in games, it’s that it doesn’t matter how good your idea is if you don’t sell it convincingly. Too many good ideas have been poorly executed or presented without the passion required to pull it over the winning line. The flip side exists, and I’ve seen an average idea being accepted because it was delivered with passion and enthusiasm. The dream has been sold, the story told, and the executives placated.


I used to struggle to sell my own ideas, low self-confidence was a constant nag. I realised that my art teacher at high school had effectively crushed my artistic musings. So, I’ve had to rebuild, to read and practise, making mistakes and reflecting on both success and failure. It may not be as tough for you; if you’re a natural you might breeze right through the process.


You may not see this initially, but part of your role is to be a salesperson, because you’re making a new product, an unseen and abstract one at this stage, often with parts no one has ever dreamt of or can even believe possible. 


An analogy might be to think of your executive team as venture capitalists, and your art team as investors, so a continual part of your role as a leader is to help sell the dream to them. When the project is fresh, everyone’s excited and times are easy. The harder times come when challenges don’t work out and problems arise, so this is where consistently showing that you’re a motivated leader really helps to pull the team along. I don’t advocate lying or fake platitudes, it’s about acknowledging when it’s hard, accepting that some are struggling, and figuring out how to collectively move forward to make what you want to be the next leading product. Be the boss you always wished you’d had.


Many of your skills will have been built from your time as a Lead running a team, so if you skipped the lead step, perhaps by going from Principal Artist to Art Director, I’d suggest reading over the topics in that section. A lot of the Art Director role builds on those foundations, from finding out about yourself, or team building, to time and process management.













5.2


Management













Team interactions 


As the head of your project’s art department, you’re going to be in a lot of meetings, and there will be a lot of discussions and a lot of art reviews. That’s all part of the job, so expect to be communicating up and down the chain in one form or another. Let’s look at who you’ll be working with and why in Figure 20.




Figure 20.
 Key relationships with the Art Director


Teams that you manage


•
 
Concept art team:
 Your visionaries. With your direction, this group helps define the look for the game team to follow. You might have daily reviews with them, looking over work as it progresses, and throwing ideas around to improve the creative process, in terms of both images and designs.


•
 
Art Leads:
 You might have one Art Lead; you might have six, depending on the size and complexity of the project and the resources available. Working with just one is a lot simpler, you work together to translate the concept artwork into engine. You’ll rely on them to work with you to figure out pipelines and iron out the kinks, to make it easier for the artists to concentrate on the making. You drive the vision; they implement it.


•
 
Artists:
 With your Leads, you’ll be reviewing the artists’ work either in person or on screen. It’s more likely that you’ll identify visual areas that need improvement or changing, while your Art Lead works out how to make that happen with the team. It’s always good to know your artists, to know each of them as a person and understand what they excel at, as well as their weaknesses. It’s not always that simple though, and as my teams grew, I found I was further away and more in higher-level meetings than with the artists, a frustrating reality of the situation.


Teams you’re not responsible for, but with whom you interact heavily


•
 
Technology and Graphics:
 You’ll want to be best buddies with these two departments, because they’ll make your vision happen. If there’s a coder who understands the artist’s mind, that’s even better; oh, the joy! I can’t tell you the difference this makes! 


The Graphics (GFX) team
 works on things like shaders, improving visual effects, volumetric lighting, new shader systems, shadows, whatever the newest and shiniest technique is. 


The Tech team
 brings new systems together, for example character customisers, planet builders, modular destruction, terrain deformation tools. It’s wholly dependent on the type of game you’re making.


•
 
Cinematic:
 The Cine team will work with your team’s assets and lighting, or they may put their own better quality lighting rigs in and add some extra meshes to raise the visual quality. Whatever their pipeline is, you’ll be part of it and if you and the cine director have a good relationship, it’ll make for a better experience. Remember, though, that there will always be some give and take.


•
 
Marketing/Publishing:
 Your company might have their own internal department in which case you’d work directly with them, or your publisher might take this role on, where you’d probably be working with their Marketing Art Director; handling promotion of the game, images, movies, press releases, social media and box/cover art, etc. 


•
 
Design:
 Your art team and the design team ideally should work together, fitting like hand in glove. There’s a complex balance between keeping the game fun and the mechanics working, but also pushing visual boundaries. It’s a constant back and forth of ideas, trying to hit the visual quality bar and keep gameplay fluid and exciting.


•
 
Narrative:
 Storytelling and world building feature heavily in the creation of the game, you’ll be working with them to develop world building, characters and reflected personalities, deciding how the key story beats influence the levels visually.


•
 
Engineering:
 For me, this relationship is the most complex of them all. This team is often hardcore, and data driven. You’ll be working with this team to consider game budgets, pipeline development, game engine boundaries and limitations, all while pushing ahead with the artwork to achieve your vision.


•
 
Human Resources:
 This division manages hiring, firing and all that stuff in between, including reviews, probation, health issues, complaints and morale. All this is quite a task list and, if you have a big team, the six-month and yearly reviews will take up a chunk of time by themselves. 


•
 
Animation:
 You'll be working closely with the animation team to provide concept designs that are both visually appealing, provide cool animation opportunities and keep the amount of technical hurdles minimal. 


•
 
Outsource (OS):
 Your project could be heavily outsourced, especially during the production phase. Your involvement will be quite high level, reviewing levels or assets that have OS content in them. Someone (probably the OS Manager) will feed your comments back to them and get the fixes in for the next delivery. Often, you’ll have a dedicated team to successfully wrangle outsource artwork, giving you time to focus on your internal team’s development.


Teams that matter but interactions are minimal


•
 
Legal:
 Often very little interaction happens with the legal team. Rarely problems arise and are often something stupid but severe in consequences. For example, someone once left a 
Star Trek
 logo on a texture and it wasn’t a 
Star Trek
 game! They got a severe reprimanding and it nearly cost them their job. With huge financial budgets involved, mistakes like this can prove extremely costly. It’s important to keep assets clean and safe; pulling unlicensed images off the internet is a quick way to get into trouble.


•
 
Executives: 
Depending on the company’s size and structure, you might meet the exec team once a week or once a year. Regardless of the frequency, always prepare well for these meetings so you can present your team’s work in the best light.


Thoughts on directing 


I thought I should collect some of my thoughts on how to be an Art Director here, and that doing so would be useful in one of two ways. Either you will think, oh, that’s cool! Or, nope, that’s terrible, I’m going to do it another way. Of course, each AD is unique and with each one comes an alternative method of delivery, but ultimately, it’s all about communication and trying to achieve what you want without the heart attack.


The cone of vision 
– This is my favourite one and I use this every day (see also 
Level 4 on Lead Artists
)
. 


Your cone of vision describes the art boundaries you are going to set, like the bumper bars in a bowling alley. The artists can bounce around and deviate to a certain degree, but ultimately, they want to hit the skittles. In this analogy, the artists need to hit the target quality, style, mood, lighting, VFX (set by previous work, a beautiful corner, or vertical slice) but also maybe something a little different or close to the concept. The result looks good, as well as achieving what art and design have stipulated, and you don’t need to micromanage.


Micromanaging
 – A trap many a first timer falls into. By micromanaging everything, not only do you become a bottleneck, but you stifle creativity. In the worst case, your team falls into a behaviour known as malicious obedience, delivering exactly what you asked and no more, knowing full well that’s not what is being asked, which creates further frustration and pressure on you and the project.


Rather than micromanaging, it’s better to understand that you can’t control everything, and something may not hit quite the quality bar you want. But ask yourself, how much does that issue really matter in the grand scope of things? Is it going to be noticed? Will it sell more games? Maybe not. 


This is not to be confused with accepting inferior quality. There is always a trade-off between the importance of the asset and whether it does what’s expected of it. Does it look good? Yes. Is it exactly what you wanted? No. How much effort, time and money do you (and can you) spend to get your vision exact? And back up a step; will it sell more games?


The f*ck theory
 – Based on the book from Mark Manson, 
The subtle art of not giving a f*ck
. When projects become stressful, workload overwhelming and you worry over the smallest of things, or worse, things that aren’t even in your control, there’s the f*ck philosophy. Everyone gives a f*ck about something, but if you’re limited to giving only three in a day, where will you spend them? Essentially this is an extreme way of letting go of some of the mental baggage we carry around, try it! It’s quite liberating, but I won’t be held accountable for any poor decision making on your part! Use with care, if overused it could be detrimental to your career.


Hot swapping
 – Sometimes an artist just can’t get to the solution you’re after. You can give them a second chance, or you can hot swap them out. Assign the task to another artist. Admittedly, I normally only use this on concept art when time is tight, and the solution isn’t being found. In this case, I’ll assign a Senior to work that tough bit out and hand it back to the original artist to implement. On one of the alien spaceships at Cloud Imperium Games, I deliberately used four different Concept Artists for different stages of the concept design process. Each artist had strengths, but time was tight, and I couldn’t afford a miss, so I made extra sure I got a hit! The lesson here is that all artists have different strengths, so utilise them accordingly and sparingly.


Jamming
 – There are times when a single concept artist isn’t hitting the high notes you need. Maybe they’re having a dreadful week, or it’s just beyond the way their brain works, or they are out of solutions. In which case, I’ll throw the entire team of say, four people, onto it and we essentially do a concept jam. We’ll all have a crack at finding a solution and see what we get. Someone will always come up with a fresh solution to the sticky problem.


Multi-shot
 – Don’t bog yourself down with absolutely having to find the solution the first time and then sticking with it. Sometimes the magic doesn’t happen, and the solution delivered is only ok, but you know there’s a better one out there. At a later time, if the project allows, go at the problem again, but only after reviewing the initial work and determining exactly why it didn’t work for you; what beats did it miss in art or design, or even animation and VFX? With this additional information, make a second pass at achieving something closer to what the project needs and which hits your quality bar.


To do any of this, though, you’ll need a team to work with. Let’s move onto some options for building out a team of artists, especially from the ground up.


Building a team 


This isn’t an exact science, at least not one for which I’ve discovered the formula. The more experience you get though, the easier it becomes. Let’s assume you’re in your first direct hiring role, having perhaps acted as second in interviews before. By that I mean that your boss would have been the lead interviewer with you as support and a second point of view, making it a more robust system for hiring the right person.


I cut my interviewing teeth at Epic, by reviewing resumes, interviewing candidates, and hiring the successful ones. With LEGO, I could shape the team, to mould it to what I felt the project needed. It was a large open-world game, but the team was largely untested on next-gen pipelines and expectations were high. At Cloud Imperium Games, this was my opportunity to build a dream team and to a large extent I did, admittedly it wasn’t all a success story, but all my Leads became Directors, so something went right.


Multiple factors influence successful hiring, though there are some common areas to consider and pitfalls to avoid.


Budget and team sizes
 – Before you can take a step towards hiring, you’ll need to have a conversation with your boss regarding how much and how many. The final figure will determine the quality of your team. If you want a crack team of artists, your monthly bills will be higher, and your headcount lower. If you flood your department with inexperienced staff, progress will be slower, and mentoring and training will take more time to level them up. It would also make the project more susceptible to mistakes, slowing everyone else down too. 


I prefer a mixed approach, with a sprinkling of Seniors and Mids forming the majority, and a steady supply of Juniors who are eager to learn and less expensive. It’s worth pointing out that the money saved in hiring will be used in training and mentoring your team. Always remember that you will need to balance time, money and quality.


Timeframe
 – Are you working for a new start-up? You’ll want to get hiring as soon as possible. This can be tough, as you’ll have no company artwork to show off, and anything you have will be subject to non-disclosure agreements (NDA). Candidates can view your own portfolio and gain an idea of the quality you represent, but it’s ultimately a big sales job.


Social media and a recruitment agency at this point will do some of the heavy lifting of attracting talent but it’s difficult to fill more senior roles. Artists are often happy and settled, so you’ll need to show them the benefit of making the switch and joining your merry new band of artists. Sell the dream!


Solid foundations
 – If you’re looking to make an instant art team, it’s easiest to pull people you already know together. This avoids the getting to know you stage, or being disappointed that someone isn’t the artist you thought they were, should you make a mistake in rapid hiring practices.


However, don’t make the mistake of hiring your friends because they’re your friends, they need to meet these requirements too:


1. Good at their job and meet the specification. 


2. You get on with them. 


3. They understand the chain of command.


Making mistakes
 – Mistakes happen, even in hiring, and it feels painful when it happens. It’s only happened to me once or twice, normally when I’ve ignored my instincts. Outside factors can influence your decision, such as pressure, bosses’ opinions, or someone with a portfolio that doesn’t quite tell the full story. Assuming you have a probation period and rigorous performance reviews, then it’s just a matter of time before it gets resolved but it can be a slow process to resolve once you go down this road.


Trust your instinct
 – Instinct is your friend, and hopefully you have learnt to listen to yours. Your years of experience will ring an alarm bell if something feels off. The only question is whether you will listen and act on it or try to push past the feeling. Ignore at your peril.


Team structure
 – Be clear on what sort of team you are trying to build, including what the biggest known hurdles are, and who you have that can provide solid answers. Fill your key roles first to get your chain of command in place. Discuss with your Leads so you all understand who does what in the early stages of team development.


Have an art test ready
 – A necessary evil. Some portfolios just don’t show enough of what you want to see. Make sure you have an art test ready to send out, which should reflect the challenges the artist is likely to face, but keep it brief. Have a list of clear instructions and deliverables, as if you were working with a contractor. That way, it’s all clear and upfront. Answer any questions they have and be supportive because you want to make a good impression on them too. 


Interviewing
 – I’ve used a few techniques, depending on the studio. For small start-ups, I like to interview the candidate but, when I’m done I’ll send in the rest of the art team (up to six) to ask some questions about their process and also get buy-in from the rest of the team. If anyone gives the thumbs down afterwards, I’ll find out why and weigh up the opinions before making the last call. When the team is large, it makes little sense for anyone other than you and the Lead Artist to interview. 


A good Lead Artist is gold
 – This is a hard role to fill, as artists don’t like to leave the safety of making cool artwork where they are, but the lure of the Lead position means more responsibility and money. They don’t have to be the best artist of the group, that’s a misunderstanding because the role requires good planning, technical and communication skills.


A good Tech Artist is like unicorn/pixie/fairy dust
 – These people are hard to find and worth hanging onto for dear life when one joins your team. If you can’t hire a dedicated person, then maybe you have someone on your project who’s attracted to the world of milliseconds, optimisation, shaders and writing scripts (not me)?


Bad attitudes
 – An artist gone bad might slip through your hiring filter, one with an ego out of control. I don’t recommend keeping hold of them, because while it’s painful to lose their experience and knowhow, it’s not worth the effect on the team. Others can fill the crater the ego leaves, to grow into and fill with the right attitude. 


Keep open minded
 – You never know where a hire might come from, what culture, what background, so keep yourself open to opportunities and remain flexible. Sometimes candidates can really surprise you with what they can deliver, given the chance.


Have someone act as a buddy
 – You’ll want some sort of settling-in system, so assign someone to take the new artist on and settle them to the team, to answer questions, solve software issues and make them feel welcome. If you can say hi and meet and greet, great, but your time will be required elsewhere, so make sure you have someone to take it on.


So, after all that you’ll have built a kick-ass team, all your positions are filled, and things are working well. Great, now let’s talk about the daily tussle of meetings versus artwork.


Balancing your working day 


On mid- to large-size art team projects, you’ll come to realise that there aren’t enough hours in the day. You can easily do an extended day, every day, say ten to twelve hours, but even then, the pile of work will always be much bigger than you can handle. Factor in that meetings will swallow at least half of your day, and suddenly your day feels even shorter. It’s easy to feel like you are climbing a mountain with no real peak in sight.


So, what can you do to combat it?


Meetings
 – You might be asking, why so many meetings? On any day, you could be holding interviews, art reviews, level reviews, senior staff play-throughs, pipeline meetings, graphics and render tech, update meetings and schedules. The bigger the game, the bigger the studio, the more meetings in my experience, unless you have found one of the few studios that operate on a more flexible system of mini meetings and often hold them at the desk.


Emails
 – Meetings generate emails. At an email heavy company, you could get 50-100 emails a day, maybe more. If you were a Lead prior to this, then chances are you already have a system in place to help with cutting down the noise. 


Come in early or go home late, I’ve always dedicated a few days a week to get to the office ahead of the team, arriving at 7am. In those first two hours I could do a normal day’s work because I was in before core hours and meetings were scheduled for core hours. If you aren’t a morning person, consider switching the other way and come in a bit later and leave late. The downside of that approach is that if you have a family, it’s harder on them.


Calendar management
 – Book time in your own calendar to create some clear-thinking time for yourself. Working from home I found also makes that process simpler.


Switching from task to task (context switching) is one of the biggest time wasters. If you can have meetings booked together and creative time blocked out before or after them, it’s better for keeping your mental/artistic flow. Using this method, decision making can be uninterrupted, and you’ll find your output is much more than with a stop-start approach.


Planning
 – Everyone wants to take a little bit of your time and if you’re not careful you’ll find yourself completely overwhelmed. It’s a dirty little secret which no one really mentions. It’s easily done when you’re hit from all sides by emails, problems, staffing, company pressure, artwork, technical decisions and scheduling. The next thing you know, you can’t think straight nor see the way forward, you make half-baked decisions without the correct information or complete picture, go home stressed and you can’t sleep. I don’t think I need to paint this picture anymore!


Having a supportive team around makes the process a whole lot smoother, giving you a chance to come up for air and start being more effective. Having a trusted Lead Artist or Producer to whom you can talk and bounce ideas off, ranging from problems or worries to schedule and timing issues, will really help reduce the pressure with the knowledge that the conversations remain confidential.


I found that writing a daily schedule helped sharpen my focus. Beginning with a quick scan of emails and making a note of any immediate tasks, then moving onto a prioritised list for that day. The aim is to get the top three done every day if possible. If I didn’t, I would roll them over to the next day and I would repeat the process.


Treat yourself like one of your team. Sometimes I’d hit problems, and I’d sit there thinking, ‘Why can’t I solve you?’ It sounds a bit crazy but here’s what I’d do to get the solution. I’d write the problem on paper. Then underneath, I’d write the question, ‘What’s the obstacle?’ This would force me to break the task down into smaller pieces. It can be as simple as writing: 


‘Plan meeting with A to discuss shader performance’ 


‘Find out problems from A’ 


‘Take info and pass to B’


Seems stupidly simple, but I found it stupidly effective.


Kiss the frog
 – From the list of priorities you’ve just generated, there will be tasks that you don’t want to do and somehow magically keep rolling over from one day to the next. What you must do is ‘kiss the frog’. You look at the horrible, ugly task that you don’t want to do, and you do it as the FIRST task of your day. Try it! It’s amazing how the pressure lifts, giving you more room to be enthusiastic about your remaining tasks.


Finding the balance between meetings to get the information you need and driving the artwork to where the team needs is a bit of a dark art, but with practice you’ll find a balance you can tolerate. 


Working with executives 


Unlike the confines of your art team, which can be all cosy and understanding, I find executives of any company work at a different level, with a different set of rules, and they take no prisoners. They can be the kryptonite to any super Art Director. Are you getting the vibe right now? Understanding how the executive team works is another skill you’ll want to hone, because getting your vision across is key to your future success.


No fluff 
– Most executives don’t seem to care about your sensitive artistic abilities. They really don’t want to know how you sweated over the light in this corner and perfectly crafted the bounced reds from the terracotta wall, how you perfectly balanced the first, second and third read forms on an elegant futuristic building, or how your team expertly engineered the most fluid yet artistic folding robot. If they don’t like it, they’ll just say no, it’s not right. It’s nothing personal, but you can’t help but take it that way sometimes.


They are your customer
 – In this situation; the customer is often right. Once in a blue moon, you might counter their argument, but mostly what they think goes. Your job is to figure out what they are looking for. It’s best to show them your ideas, giving them options to mull over. Often executives are untrained in how to describe why the art isn’t working for them, but they know what they want and what they expect to see/feel. 


‘No’ is not an option
 – No, nein, nada, whatever your language, leave it at the meeting room door. This team wants options. Even if you hate those options, give them several, and if you don’t, they’ll start making them up, which can make directing your game much harder than it already is.


Do your homework
 – You can’t turn up to an exec meeting and hope to bluster your way through it, because they’ll pick you to pieces. Spend time learning your pitch, anticipate their questions, and show confidence in your designs and clarity in your thinking, so that even if they don’t like your ideas, if you have a solid logic behind them, at least you’ll feel ok if they shoot you down.


Time is precious
 – Avoid waffle and give some context to the talk, mentioning any known caveats upfront, thus wasting no time talking about something that’s not ready to be worried over. Be prepared for interruptions and segues and always try to bring it back on track.


Personalities
 – Often your opposite on the character wheel, these people like specifics and solid data. If your schedule has holes in it, they’ll spot them. Work with your producer to make sure everything is well crafted and be prepared to be flexible. Be in no doubt that options and ideas will be presented that add more work and cut down scheduled time, a weird paradox you’ll have to fight.


Pats on the back
 – Are few and far between. These meetings are about facts, not feelings, and this team cares about the product, its schedule and market share. They want to know how long the levels will take to design, why the outsource studio is failing, whether the freelancer rates are too high or staffing too low, and whether to put a freeze on hiring. All this makes the company tick. Patting you on the back is the last thing on their minds.


Winning
 – Is coming out unscathed. You live to fight another day, to practise and sharpen your skills for the next time. You find out how they operate and what they expect, and you deliver a stronger presentation next time.


Confidence and planning are key because this group means business and so should you. It might feel alien to you initially, but over time it’ll get a little easier. Your exposure to the exec team might be once a week, might be once every quarter, but in between, you have the business of making artwork.













5.3


Art process













Reference gathering 


Sadly, the magic ‘make-art’ button doesn’t exist, but fear not, there is a loose process we can follow. Step one of the make-art process begins with doing your homework. This means doing research and gathering inspiration, which we’ll cover more in the 
Exploration section
 of ‘Putting it all together’. Whether you’re collating images, movies, stories, or technical documents doesn’t really matter; they’re all forms of communicating your visual storytelling guide.


As we’ve seen, the games industry is highly collaborative. But to collaborate you must communicate effectively. Knowing how and what to communicate to your team and at what time isn’t as clear-cut and simple as it sounds. The difficulty is there are so many ways, and so much information, to communicate to different people at different stages. I think that’s what I failed to understand early in my Art Director (AD) career. Understanding how to communicate clearly through visuals is another art. 


Can too much reference be a bad thing?


I used to cover inspiration boards with all sorts of cool imagery. Often images would appear to contradict each other. I knew what I was thinking and why the images were there, but it was a shotgun approach and it was missing a vital ingredient – clarity. 


Two people can look at the same image and focus on different elements. Imagine a rusty red farm tractor, but what’s the focus unless it’s called out? Is it the shade of red paint, the scratches in the paint, or the proportion of red paint to grey metal? Which is it? Without clarity, artists will rely on mind reading, which as far as I know is a myth!


There’s a little piece of me that hates writing this. I can’t quite believe that’s how I operated, but I now know why; I was hesitant to put my stamp on artwork to commit. I wanted to give artists flexibility, to give them ownership, but it didn’t help me, and it didn’t help them when they focused on the wrong thing. I needed to provide more consistency with clear direction. 


Provide a clear framework


You want your artists to flex their creative muscles, to give them the opportunity to grow. However, it’s a balancing act to find the middle ground where you provide clear direction in concept art with clear intent but also leave space for the artists to fill by themselves.


Taking your reference images and categorising them into areas of design language including complexity, technology, level of advancement, materials and lighting, along with the reasons you have picked them, will go a long way towards reducing confusion. Your team will have a better idea of why you’re pushing in a particular direction, and with that knowledge, they can help push in the same direction too, rather than fighting it. Building on that base, the creative decisions from the artists will be more cohesive and in-line with your overall vision.


‘Not this’


A simple but super effective method; in fact I find it just as useful as a whole reference board. Dedicating a section of your mood board or style guide, providing images labelled, ‘Not this’ is a clear way of saying, don’t do this, don’t use these colours, don’t use this type of clothing manufacturing, and steer clear of this type of material or lighting. My advice is to try it and see how it completes the circle. If you’ve clearly stated what to do and what not to do, there’s no room for confusion and wasted work.


Deconstruction  


As an Art Director you’ll often be faced with assets that you’ve never worked on, maybe even new genres and styles. As part of your toolkit, it’s important to look at someone’s artwork or a design and be able to reduce it down to its components. The ability to deconstruct an image or design is vital to your success.


Asking questions, I find, is the best way. Let’s take an FPS weapon as an example. Ask yourself, what are the features of this weapon?


•
 How does it work? 


•
 What makes it effective as a design? 


•
 Which parts are important to its usefulness? 


•
 How is its manufacturing process affecting its visual design?


•
 Is there anything considered unusual? 


•
 What role does it play in real life? 


•
 What adjectives would you use to describe it?


Then factor in deconstructing the visuals, looking at the proportions, the materials used, the number of materials used, the wear, construction (bolts, welded, press-fit), how it animates and how much animation there is, whether it has attachments all contribute to the discovery process.


Whether you are deconstructing an object or a piece of concept art, the aim is to define what makes it, ‘it’. With that knowledge, you can apply it to new artwork (not copying), improving and advancing in new directions, remixing it and adding your own stamp. You are a reverse engineer in the making! 


The same but different


The temptation for new ADs when developing new assets is to reference existing artwork. While that’s great for deconstruction, and awareness, of your competitors and certainly recommended, the question remains, how to break into fresh territory? 


As an AD, you’re constantly looking for the visual hook; what’s going to grab and keep players’ attention? What will they remember about this game when describing it to their friends? It’s especially important for environments but also applies to all other assets. This is a delicate balance of merging the familiar with the fresh, something the player will appreciate visually but also be comfortable with from a usability point of view.


I have a few go-to websites for daily inspiration, which I have pinned on my web browser, so I don’t forget them. I look over them daily to get fresh ideas, to keep my inspiration up and the grey matter still sparking away. My inspiration boards feed into different assets, and not just related to the type of game I’m making, including architecture, UI, VFX and industrial design. Whichever way you achieve it, broadening your knowledge, expanding your mental toolkit and improving your chances of finding your ‘aha moment’ are key to pushing your artistic boundaries.


Image/asset libraries and sources


Time pressure is ever present in game development. The pressure to perform and to hit high quality bars in a timely manner can lead some companies to make costly mistakes. The ideal situation is for the company’s artists and Concept Artists to use images, textures, materials and assets with the correct copyright permissions when they want to reduce creation time by using wholly, or part of, someone else’s asset. 


It’s best to have a library of these assets on your server and, only after the legal department has given the go ahead, to purchase and use them.  


As an AD, you must be on your toes and work with other departments to make sure the whole art team is clear on what is acceptable and safe to use. It’s ok to have random images from the internet on your reference/inspiration board, but that’s as far as it goes. 


Once you have your reference boards, your concept team can start developing their ideas, using them as a guide. At the start of a project in pre-production, your reference and mood boards form an early part of a style guide.


Art bible and style guides 


An art bible is a living reference document to describe how the game is visually assembled. Over the course of the project, the bible slowly expands and fills out to become more and more specific, providing artists with detail on every part of the project. It’s not a fast, ‘just add water and watch grow’ kind of process. It takes time to piece everything together, and like the art assets, over time it becomes more and more refined.


In Figures 21 and 22, I’ve created a fictional game set on a single human world. It has a bit of everything! As you can see, each asset type (creatures, weapons, environments, etc) is essentially a chapter of the art bible containing specific information to each type of asset.




Figure 21.
 The chapters of a game art bible


Taking one of these asset chapters, you can further break it down into descriptive elements.




Figure 22.
 Examples of an art bible chapter


Manufacturer
 – This text is often provided by the narrative team, and might be something like this: ‘This manufacturer makes the crazy space pirate, low-tech looking weapons, prone to overheating, cobbled together from scavenged pieces’. From this you have your starting point and overall direction.


Silhouettes
 – You might have multiple weapons, each filling a certain expected class. Games often replicate the real world in their function, close combat (shotgun or pistol), mid-range (small machine gun or assault rifle), long range (sniper), heavy fast and inaccurate (heavy machine gun), long distance (rocket launcher). Each weapon is expected to fill its role not only functionally, but also as a quick visual read. The goal is to quickly look at this weapon in the chaos of game playing and understand its function. If your rocket launcher is the same size as your pistol, people will have a hard time accepting it unless the game is humorous, and size/power is the joke.


Colour palette
 – What colours will define this weapon? Is it the black and moody one with glowing blue energy? Is it the chrome with green glowing parts? 


Animation
 – What kind of animation is expected? Crazy, over-complicated with multiple moving parts, or more traditional, some recoil, some overheating?


Material types
 – Is the weapon high quality or low-tech? In our example, the weapon is scavenged from the world around you and therefore can be a mix of high- and low-tech. The classic is to take a conventional weapon and tape some high-tech equipment to it, with a few loose wires and someone’s name scratched into the wood to indicate prior ownership and enrich the storytelling. So, you might choose to feature wood, rusted metal, some plastic for the wires and some old broken 3D-printed plastics.


VFX
 – When the weapon fires, reloads or overheats, what kind of visual effect will you see? Steam, vented plasma, sparks? When the weapon projectile hits something, what does that look like? What decal does it leave behind as a burn mark? How do they interact with the environment? These questions can be answered here.


Not this
 – Optional but encouraged. A section to highlight what this weapon isn’t. For example, it will never be polished chrome, no brass, the construction isn’t clean and precise, no carbon fibre or leaking oil.


Art is a journey of discovery 


You don’t need to choose everything explicitly from the start and chain yourself to your original decisions, because it’s too limiting, and you want the freedom to explore and change course if something’s not working. That’s the nature of being a creative. Naturally, you’ll have some boundaries in place, a direction to follow, but the path you’re taking isn’t fully realised. 


By the time you hit production, you’ll have your tech, your vertical slice and your beautiful corners, and then you can start locking down the theory of your artwork. Then you’ll know what works, the forms you’ll use and where, which technology and why, bolts or no bolts, the scale, the lighting, the mood, this flower and tree goes with this soil type, and so on.


I suspect a lot depends on your personality and the project’s history (if it has any). Bibles are notoriously hard to keep updated, and there’s a dichotomy in that you can really only make your bible when you are in full development, but when you’re in full development, who has the time to be making art bibles while the pressure is building and deadlines are demanding? I guess the solution, like all of game making, is again to take it one bite at a time. 


My advice is not to aim to make the mother of all art bibles, because often the workload is huge and would require a dedicated team. Aim to make smaller bite-size chapters, starting with the fundamentals and broad themes, then slowly focus on the detail. Bit by bit, piece by piece, eventually you’ll have a full bible, chapter, and verse.


I’ve yet to work at a company with anything that resembles a complete art bible or style guide. Mostly the guide is kept as a collective bank of knowledge passed from generation to generation of artists, like a folktale. Perhaps this is because I’ve not worked at large multi-national companies and have leaned more towards independent studios. 


The moral of the story is that it’s great to have an up-to-date style guide if you can. You’ll need something in place for the team to reference. The question of how complete it’ll be at any one time is up to you to answer.


Art directing Concept Artists 


As the Art Director (AD), you oversee all the artists in the team, however there’s one group that is the tip of the spear, at the forefront of this journey, namely the artists who hack, slash and create new ground for others to build on. Concept Artists are a pivotal part of game development who, with your direction, create a vision out of thin air. Where once there was a blank layer in Photoshop, now there’s a whole new world, one that everyone wants to build.


The type of company, game, and whether it’s a new IP or a sequel, will demand different types of artists. We’ll cover these a little further on, so when you’re building your team you can hire the right people for the project.


Some projects are on rails. Each successive game is an incremental update. As a new AD on an established team, the Concept Artists carry more of the game DNA than you, and they could probably do it without you. But the role of the AD is to energise the concept team, to drive them to new ideas, pushing them outside their comfort zone to deliver exciting artwork for the team to follow.


The opposite end of the spectrum is the new IP, the brand-new, from-the-ground-up game project. There’s no artwork to reference from previous projects, so it’s both challenging and daunting for you and the concept team, requiring more experienced artists to create the foundation artwork for the game and less experienced artists to fill in the smaller undefined areas. In my experience, the new IP requires a strong, experienced AD.


With UT3, on the one hand, we had a lot of creative freedom, but on the other, we were bound by the conventions of the universe. In the later parts of the project, I would feed ideas to the concept team, but to be honest, they could do the project with their eyes closed and were fully able to direct the art themselves. Later on they gave me a caretaker role as an AD on a new IP idea. Looking back, it was a test, one that I failed. My skills weren’t developed enough to take on a brand-new IP with the full weight and expectation of Epic Games. It was however, a lesson that I took very much to heart, it has informed the AD I am today. 


LEGO was a simpler proposition for me. I’d learnt some new skills by then in communication, leading and directing by example after my time at Rocksteady. LEGO City Undercover was part of an established IP, the style set. Work had already begun when I got there, it was the perfect introduction to art direction as I see it now. Again, the concept team knew the style of the project inside out, but had less experience in visually developing next-gen level art, which is something I loved doing.


Star Citizen was a whole other level of difficult. Over time, I've really come to appreciate the different styles, artistic quality and level of detail needed for each stage of development in this demanding sci-fi universe. 


As you’ve probably figured out, different projects put different demands on you and affect what type of Concept Artist you need. Some games are easier than others no doubt, but that doesn’t make art directing easy. It is important you surround yourself with the best Concept Artists you can afford (if that’s an option), people who fit the brief. Let’s break this down further.


What type of Concept Artist do you need?


On hiring, you’ll be making a call on what type of Concept Artist you want. Concept Artists come in many varieties, but there are two broad types:


•
 
Generalist
 – Especially for small game teams and when building up a studio, it’s important to hire a heavy hitter, an artist who’s a generalist with a broad expanse of work and who understands worlds and characters, props and vehicles, weapons and even VFX. Often this will be someone experienced with an established career, someone flexible and able to contribute wherever needed. They are the backbone of your concept team. 


•
 
Specialist
 – When you really need top quality work, you’ll want to use a specialist, whether they specialise in worlds, weapons or characters. I use specialists for spaceships and weapons to get the high quality the project demands. Because of their experience, you spend less time in iterations and more time working towards a completed concept design. The final delivery of artwork is higher quality too, often with more out-of-the-box thinking incorporated into the model and images.


What type of art do you need?


Your project has multiple stages of development. We’ve covered those in the Junior Artist section as part of the pipeline. Likewise, concept art has different stages. I can’t speak for other ADs, but I’ve found that each type of concept art style has its place for your project. Project budget will be one of the defining factors, because more time equals more money, which makes sense, right? But you can increase speed and reduce quality, to remain within budget.


I’ve worked on projects where the final concept design is so detailed, the artwork is practically a blueprint. This is especially useful if your team is less experienced and the type of artwork is complex. On the other hand, I’ve worked on games where the concept art is limited in volume and the imagery describes more the mood, atmospherics, and lighting, with a looser understanding of the architecture, props, weapons and characters. It’s a faster way of working, it leaves more to the imagination and requires a stronger, more experienced art team. Let’s look at some of the common working methodologies, which I like to call the 
THREE Ds
. 


Discovery
 – As an art technique, it’s best described as fast and loose. This technique isn’t limited to the start of a project and can be employed at any time throughout the process. It’s about communicating a mood, a feeling or concept. The idea is to complete the task quickly and roughly, almost like sketching on paper and moving onto another piece of paper, and so on. No one thing is more precious than the other, the loose lines or geometry help define the scale, palette, or mood, and the Concept Artist isn’t slowed down with details. 


The benefit is greater breadth of exploration in the time frame. It’s wide, not deep. Images will be hard to read in some areas, with patches blending into another, crossing boundaries. The same goes for geometry, it might be filled with dirty meshes, intersecting, stretched and scaled, all roughly assembled, and often a mix of the two. This enables the Art Director and the senior staff to see ideas quickly, feeding back and iterating quickly to hit upon new and exciting designs.


Development
 – The natural progression to developing a final concept image. While some teams can cope with discovery art, I prefer to continue developing the artwork into something more precise and clear. Once you’ve hit upon some ideas you’re happy with, it’s beneficial to spend further time refining the concept. Either you or your Concept Artists go back to the start and rebuild the artwork cleanly, taking the time to work out some more of the problematic areas. 


Often game design requirements have greater influence at this stage too. Not only does it have to look cool, but also solve aspects of gameplay; they want a more prominent tower, a way of getting up the cliff-side easily, more mood with pools of light to give the character stealth advantages, add more armour to the character, and so on. These are factored into the final image.


Detail
 – The last stage of the concept process, which is defined by the type of asset to a certain degree. If we assume the image shows a small town, surrounded by rock-faces, with characters holding ornate staffs, riding on alien-looking animals, then the final stage would be a ‘break out’ image. 


Each asset listed would get its own image, breaking down in detail the assets you’re looking at to define material break-up, animations, proportions, variations, examples of real-world reference, scale compared to a human, or different angles. Not everyone provides this stage and the range of what’s delivered varies, but it provides the ultimate level of direction for your artists when supplied.


Developing concept art is a team effort, one in which you push the concept team, they push you, and you all advance together. With the right team and the right attitude, it’s amazing what you can conjure up, and then refine and finally detail. Having a concept team in-house I think is still the best plan for a studio’s success. The knowledge remains in-house and the vision of the game more consistent. 


It is possible to operate a concept team built from freelancers, but only if you have the time and the resources to keep design and feedback on track. I’ve been doing a mixture of both, so read on to find out more.


Freelance Concept Artists 


Trying to hire full-time in-house Concept Artists can be a difficult proposition. Oftentimes, the good ones are already working for another company or they’re happy freelancing. So, if you can’t tempt them away from their current job, what options do you have?


You could hire Juniors and work from the ground up, but often the work is too complex and requires experienced artists to get the right results for you and your studio. But when time is short, can you afford to keep missing the mark? Another option is to get external help, in the guise of freelancers.


Why use a freelancer?  


Depending on budget, you can run one or more freelancers in your team. The upside is that you can afford to specialise, to use artists according to their strengths, while the downside is that it’s more to manage and takes more time to get each one acclimatised to the project, the style and the philosophy behind it. When you get a good, strong working relationship with someone, it’s worth holding onto. 


What are the benefits of an agency? 


An agency is a small outsource studio, normally specialising in creating concept art. The upside is they often provide a more complete service, assigning an internal Art Director and Producer to the project to relieve some pressure. The downside is the work costs more, and rates can vary depending on the studio. 


Why use top-tier talent? What do you get? 


With Concept Artists, you get what you pay for. With the higher price tag, they can bring a wealth of experience, providing quick iteration and out-of-the-box thinking, making visual leaps that you never even thought of. Beware though, as some artists have a high price tag but not the experience to fully back up the hefty day rate; be sure to look at their time in the industry and the variety of work they have provided previously.


What can go wrong?


If you don’t plan, almost everything and anything can and will go wrong. Work with your outsource manager and/or the legal team to get schedules, details, Plan B, pipeline and assets all ready before investing in a contract. Arrange some time to meet and greet, find out about them, let them know about you and how you work. No one likes surprises.


How much can you expect to pay?


Artists generally have two rates. One is a day rate and the other, a long-term rate. Let’s say you only want a week or two’s worth of work, then in that case you will normally pay the higher day rate. For longer-term work, the studio can normally negotiate a lower day rate as you’re guaranteeing work and providing a more stable work life for the artist. 


You are the client 


I like to work with contract artists and treat them much as I do the internal team; holding regular reviews, regular work updates and bouncing ideas back and forth, because I want them to be invested to get the best work of them. If you act like an unreasonable boss, making last minute unreasonable demands, it doesn’t bode well for a future working relationship.


My contract artist didn’t deliver the expected level of work. What happens now?
