That depends, and the reasons can be many. It’s not fair to expect an artist to work on a new complex project and hit a home run straight away. There’s a bedding-in period. If the contract is short, I find the best way is to help it along to get the results you want, so think of it as a collaborative process in which two minds are better than one. Keep to the cone of vision method and course correct where needed but try to keep them invested. If the artist just can’t hit the standards or provide the answers, then you know for next time what their strengths and weaknesses are (if there is a next time). If the artist plain old strikes out, speak to your outsourcing manager and legal team.


In the past I’ve found we achieved the best results collaboratively; it takes more work though and is something you should consider before using a freelancer on a contract. If you can’t direct the freelancer, is there someone else who can? Maybe your Principal, Lead, or assistant Art Director if you have one? Whichever you decide, consider it carefully, it’s important to find the right artist to suit the role.


Art directing game art team 


Part of your role is to collaborate with your concept team, to work as a cohesive unit to clearly communicate ideas. Another major part is your team of game artists, hungry to get creating, to translate all the crazy and wonderful ideas into the game engine, leading to making ‘The best game of all time’. 


Once the ball starts rolling and game assets are being built, you’ll find yourself dedicating more and more time to review sessions with your art team. The point of all the reviews? To gather intel and to evaluate the game, ensuring that the artwork fits the game’s narrative and meets your expectations of style and design. 


Communication and feedback prove time and time again to be vital parts of the art pipeline, with defined stages of development: White box, Grey box, Final art and Polish, providing formal opportunities for review and feedback with the artists and your Lead. Don’t limit your feedback to just these key stages if possible; if you see something going off track and looking weird, don’t hang around. Talk to your Lead first if it’s something major, or make suggestions to your artists directing them on where to adjust if it’s minor.  


Let’s look at the various options for giving feedback to your team: 


•
 
Weekly Review
 – Once a week or once a fortnight, you and your art team all meet up (in person or video conference) and show your progress in the form of your latest and greatest artwork or work in progress. It’s a chance for everyone to see what’s happening and especially important if you are working in a distributed studio (everyone in a different place).


For you and your Lead Artist, this is a great opportunity to help drive the vision, to give motivation to the team or nods of approval, to maintain coherence. Some companies will roll two meetings into one, having the show and tell, plus quick critique sessions. It’s also a good time to pass on project information, updates or have a Q&A session, giving your artists some well-deserved Art Director attention.


•
 
Leads review
 – Let’s assume you have a team of Leads: Lead Artist, Lead Tech Artist, Lead Animator (in some companies you might be the Director of Animation), Lead VFX, Lead Character, Lead Vehicle, Lead Weapon, Lead Cinematic and Senior Environment Lead. 


With a team this big, you might want to meet once a week to keep lines of communication open and information flowing smoothly. When a project is running at full speed, it’s easy for your Leads to become isolated and work in their own bubbles. Regular review is a way of bringing them all together, to discuss any high-level issues and solve problems as the project develops. Alternatively, you might have just one art Lead and with you, they oversee all the art department. In which case, meetings can be less formal and more ad hoc.


That was my situation at Epic Games. I really enjoyed my weekly, and sometimes, daily, check-ins with my Art Director, chatting about games, life and all in between. If you have a good, strong, trusting relationship with your Lead, it improves your game development life massively.


•
 
Artist desk review 
– Assuming your team is small enough and you have the time, it’s always great to go around and see what they are doing, chat to the artists, giving them a boost and a push at the same time. It’s a way of seeing first-hand any new techniques and giving you more of an insight for when you meet with your Leads.


•
 
At desk on your own 
– At some point, you’ll find time (you have blocked in some time, correct?) where you can sit down at your desk (or stand), put your headphones on and spend some quality time reviewing the current state of the game. You might want to walk about the environment, evaluating flow, lighting, asset quality or looking ahead and identifying areas of improvement to implement for the next milestone. How you spend your time is largely up to you.


I spent hours reviewing levels and artwork at my desk on my own. On a smaller game it’s a simpler task, while on large open-world games it’s much more demanding. I would take screen shots, annotate them and drop them into a large document, collating everything for my Lead Artist or outsource company to assign to the relevant artists. Naturally, it’s only worth doing this at certain stages, otherwise you feel like you’ll go insane!


Outsourcing art assets 


Sometimes, your studio needs help. Faced with accelerated timelines, finding new staff can be a slow and frustrating process, especially if you just don’t have the office space or resources to cope with expanding your team. So, what do you do? The natural next step is to bring in some additional expertise. It’s a well-trodden path, with many well-known AAA companies employing the services of an outsource (OS) studio to boost their art and level content.


I worked with OS studios at Epic, I also worked and helped train a team of artists in Shanghai as part of building out the Unreal Tournament 3 universe. I can say it was a once-in-a-lifetime opportunity and well worth the experience. Once the pipeline is running smoothly, it feels good to see your game grow and develop as the new assets get delivered and integrated into the game build. 


It can take a while to iron out the kinks in your art pipeline and it’s easy to get carried away, hoping that OS will be the silver bullet to your tricky problems. So that your company doesn’t throw good money after bad, I’ve noted a few thoughts on the process for you to think over.


When to outsource
 – This depends on what you’re outsourcing. Concept art can be done at any time. For in-game assets, I find outsourcing best in the middle of the project, where everything is a known quantity. For marketing, it’s towards the final stages of the game.


Set up your pipeline
 – Before you do anything, be clear on how to make your own assets. If you don’t, you’ll be spending extra time and money as your OS studio completes research and development at your expense. For the process to work best, make sure your art team knows how to build the assets from start to finish, and that your tech artists have given the thumbs up and engineering is also on board with the plan. 


Documentation
 – Provide clear documentation for creating assets, both artistically and technically, explaining the full process, file structure, naming conventions, shader set-up, rigging requirements, bone counts, physics proxies, draw calls, vertex counts, dimensions, Photoshop file structure, source files, delivery methods – it’s extensive! It can also double up as documentation for your own internal art team.


Choose your OS partner wisely
 – Start by interviewing them like you would a team member. If you have an OS manager, they’ll find you candidates and you’ll review finalists. As part of the process, you’ll want to find out how they operate in terms of team sizes, capacity, iteration process, feedback process, method of delivery, cost and contracts. 


Often companies will complete a test asset for you, based on your requirements. If the test asset is amazing quality, you’ll want to know if the artist(s) involved will be assigned to the team making your artwork; the last thing you want is subpar artwork after the contract is complete.


Set you expectations
 – Specify how you expect assets to be delivered, as you don’t want new assets breaking the game build and slowing the project down. Identify how you’ll work together when things go wrong, how to fix issues, and how many times an asset can be corrected.


Home team
 – Have a dedicated team for managing the OS pipeline in your studio, too. They can concentrate on the schedule, payments, the delivery process and validation of assets. Ideally, you’ll want a dedicated team which is able to review, review, test and give feedback on delivered assets. They’re the first line of defence, checking the basics before it even gets to you and your Lead Artist. Hire specifically for these roles as the workload can be extensive and is not to be underestimated.


I’ve worked with a few OS companies; it was a learning-on-the-job kind of gig, having never done it before nor having someone to show me the ropes. I won’t be so bold to say these are the golden rules to follow, but they’ll definitely give you something to think about if you’re considering some extra art muscle.













5.4


Putting it all together













Making the magic happen 


Making a game isn’t, as you now realise, an exact science (as much as the finance department would like it to be) and dependencies on budget, team size, experience, location, project type and timeline all factor into the equation. The scale of your company’s ambition will dictate how long you and your team have to craft the next masterpiece.


If you only have a year to make a game, you’re going to have to work hard and smart. If you have many years (often six or above for new, large IPs) then, theoretically, you can afford more time for exploration of your artistic muse, but you’ll still be under pressure to deliver.


As I mentioned, art direction is fluid, it twists and turns and everyone appears to have a unique method for solving their challenges. I don’t profess to have the secret sauce to everything director related, but I’ll give you my best shot, passing on what I’ve figured out in my journey, which I hope can give you a bit of a head start.


Until now we’ve done a lot of preparation to understand what underlies being an Art Director. We’ve built upon everything learnt in the previous roles up to the Lead Artist section, so now you’ve got the tools, you’ve got the supplies, you just need the final piece of the puzzle in this crazy career guide, starting with the exploration phase, taking you all the way to release and DLC.


What follows explains the high-level process, which from start to finish gives you an idea of the cycle an Art Director will experience, and is tied into the project pipeline steps we established earlier on. It’s not an explicit ‘how to art direct a game’ but more of a guided tour. 




Figure 23. 
The art direction process at each stage of the game development pipeline


Exploration (planning phase) 


Exciting times! A new game, new opportunities, new game mechanics, new worlds and the chance to push boundaries of creativity, it sounds like the perfect job. But where are the boundaries, how do you know how far to push, do you break through the confines or stay within the walls? Well, that depends on your project and the requirements of gameplay. We might not like to admit it, but gameplay and design play a large part of the Art Director’s decision-making process in this planning stage.


The creative cabal


You’ll be working with the Creative and Design Directors initially. They’re responsible for the game idea as gatekeepers to this new world, but that doesn’t mean you’re not part of the club. It’s a collaborative process and, while you’re taking your cues from the project’s needs, it’s still down to you (and your concept team) to develop new visual ideas, often feeding back into the game design, improving and opening up fresh thinking.


As part of the cabal, there’ll be a narrative team or a writer (if it’s a small team) who works with the Creative Director to develop and advance the game’s storyline (presuming it has one). All the time, the foundation is being laid, forming the initial boundaries of the universe you’re working in, setting some loose fundamentals for you to bounce off.


Fresh or familiar ground


While the new game IP may be the most exciting stage, it’s the most challenging for an Art Director. Simpler (but still challenging) would be the existing IP, and if you’re an untested Art Director, theoretically, this is your best path for entry.


It ain’t all about art


Before we get carried away and launch into talking artwork, let’s stop and consider the game tech and its implications. Games are much quicker to make if you work with existing game and editor tech, updating the art, creating some new assets and levels, sprinkle on a few new game play mechanics, throw it into the microwave, ding, game ready. 


Game development really slows down when large amounts of R&D are required, which can add years to a game. You might get lucky (depends on who you ask) and be working with new hardware, say a new generation of a game console that opens opportunities for creativity. Not only that, game engines are increasingly powerful with each iteration, enabling the entire philosophy of ‘If you can imagine it, you can create it’. The difficulty is knowing where to draw the line!


Gather the forces


Ok, we’ve delayed enough, let’s talk about the creative process, beginning with exploring your many sources of artwork reference. This stage is for exploration, to expand the horizons of the project, starting with what you like and know well, then pushing out from there. Some companies will want you to stay within the lines, no colouring outside nor overstepping your boundary; others will want the opposite and expect more thinking outside the box. Hopefully, you’ll already know what type of artist you are and will work to your strengths. 


It’s the Art Director’s decision where and when to pull in extra support. A project can be a collaboration of artistic ideas, but ultimately, you decide which ideas you place in front of the other directors. Importantly, like your adventure so far, there isn’t just one path, and you’ll be expected to provide multiple artistic options for the Creative Director to look over. Maybe a few directions are chosen to explore further, as there’s no use restricting yourself to one path. At this stage, it’s about creating choice.


If you are anything like me, you’ll already have a folder crammed with links, animations and images. I always say, see an image, save it. I know everything exists on the internet, but sometimes it can prove impossible to find that one image that would be perfect for your project if only you could find it again. 


Concept art (pre- and production phases) 


Hands down, the initial exploratory concept phase is among my favourites. It’s always a wonder to look at a piece of work and think ‘A week ago, this didn’t exist’, and the next thing you know, you are running, jumping and flying around it! But let’s not get ahead of ourselves, let’s answer a fundamental question first:


What’s concept art and what’s the point? 


Concept art represents an idea, a vision. It’s the manifestation of thought, form, function, storytelling and world building. It’s a way of communicating to create a reference point. When someone creates a concept image, it provides direction to everyone on the team. It’s a highly effective method of refining your vision, slowly polishing until it’s crystal clear. 


How do you use the team to their best ability? 


We’ve already established the foundations of the world set by the narrative and design teams. At this point you’ve gathered a wide array of ideas, conferred with other directors, and have some choices in the direction to follow. After that, it’s just the simple matter of hitting the ‘Go’ button, opening the creative gates and letting your imagination, and that of your concept team run wild.


You’ll use the three stages I described earlier in this level to develop the visual themes of the project. Discovery, development, and detail, in that order initially. Creating a visual framework for the game art team to follow, with each stage of the process solving unanswered questions about gameplay, design and art theory. The concept art team isn’t always at the sharp end of visual development. They’re woven into the development process for the length of the project and will appear over and over again.


As the project develops, transitioning from the stages of White box, Grey box and Final art, it’ll become clear that some areas of the game need revision; it could be scene composition, mood and lighting, balance of materials, or visual complexity. Whatever it is, between you and the concept team, you’ll use one of the three techniques to solve the problem. 


How many concept artists for the team?


The size and demands of the project affect this answer. If you have a small and tight budget, chances are that you and a single Concept Artist are doing the work. If both the art team and the budget are big, there’ll be big expectations, so you might have a bigger internal team of Concept Artists to work with. You may also need to use an outsource studio or group of freelancers to help hit your deadlines and visual targets.


As an Art Director, it’s about getting the result you want. Which, ultimately, is a ‘big old win’. You might have a solid internal concept team, but they might lack some skills or be early in their careers. While you’d like to give them the opportunity to grow, the pressure of schedules means that sometimes you need the help of freelance heavy hitters, artists who can create amazing images, be innovative and hit their timelines. Different artists create different types of images, so be mindful of what artworks are needed. Is it fast and loose, slow and defined, methodical and technical? Choose according to the problems you’re solving.


How much concept art does a project need? 


There’s no definitive answer to this question. The size, scope, complexity and budget of the project will help shape the answer. Small games often will have one or two Concept Artists, while large projects can require internal teams of eight to ten with additional support from freelancers when needed.


Do you use all the artwork? 


No, you don’t. A lot of it can be unused, but it’s a necessary part of the exploration process. With each page of designs, you are testing what works, what’s too much or too little, and then feeding back to the artists for their next iteration.


What happens if you can’t find a solution? 


Sometimes you can’t get the solution you want. It exists in your mind’s eye but on screen, is frustratingly lacking something. Often, with additional probing questions about design or functionality, feeling or readability, you arrive at the destination. Sometimes though, you might just need to switch the process up. If your Concept Artist has hit the limits of their current skills, and you have the knowledge and skills elsewhere, take their work and use it as a springboard to achieving something you are satisfied with.


If the subject is beyond your skill set, pass it to someone with the knowhow to solve the problem. Don’t be afraid to mix it up and hire a freelancer if your internal team can’t deliver, use their artwork as further direction for your internal team. Do whatever you need to unblock the concept art pipe.


On Unreal Championship 2, we had two Concept Artists, both highly skilled, one worked on paper and scanned his images in, another worked in Photoshop and 3DSMax. Between them, they created almost all the arenas, weapons, characters and pick-ups. For Unreal Tournament 3, we had one Concept Artist internally who focused on characters, weapons and vehicles and we relied on intermittent external artwork for the arenas. 


On Batman and LEGO games, there were just two Concept Artists for both projects. Both were big games with massive amounts of artwork, but the game art team used their imagination to fill in the many blanks!


On Star Citizen, we’ve created more concept artwork than anywhere I’ve experienced, high quality, high quantity and highly detailed. Working mainly in 3D, it enables the development process from idea into game asset to be faster and more accurate as the artists use the concept art as a template.


Are you starting to see a pattern here? Games and studios work in unique ways to keep that all-important agility. In a nutshell, this is the process I’m used to, and of course, it’s far more nuanced than what I’ve described, but this is the framework in which everyone bounces around, examining problems, and finding visual solutions. 


Game art (pre- and production phases) 


Making pretty pictures is one thing, but that doesn’t make a game does it? Well, maybe one day it will, when we can use AI to translate the visuals, but for now it’s all down to the art team to make sense of the concept art and build game engine–ready assets. It’s one thing to see a cool image and think, ‘Oh yeah, looks awesome,’ but ultimately it must support the game design and narrative. So, once you have some concept art that you’re happy with, what happens next?


In pre-production, you normally start with a small, tight team. While you are in this exploratory phase, it’s better to keep your artistic agility up, maintaining a small core group who work together to nurture the new title. This merry band normally will be composed of Senior Artists, a Principal, a Tech Artist, and an Animator too, each team member will be multi-skilled, able to tackle a wide variety of artistic problems; think of them as an artistic elite squad, no problem is too big!


Team size – big or small?


This is the classic project management triangle; scope, cost and time all factoring into deciding the size of your art team. 


My preference is to have a strong internal team based in one studio. The biggest team I worked with was forty artists, and the smallest five. The hardest challenge I’ve faced was building a studio’s art team from the ground up while we were still a start-up with no previous game to show. Another challenge was that the company ballooned in size at the same time and we had to deliver content regularly, while not having tech or staff in place. Now that was a mountain to climb!


Prototypes


The aim of the game is to find out if your team’s concept art ideas work in-game and will iterate rapidly, using a quick cycle of implement, test, feedback and repeat. 3D concepts work best in this situation; as soon as the concept model is complete, the art team can import the geometry into the game editor and test away.


This type of workflow enables directors to quickly see versions of the concept art and decide if it meets the game’s brief, which is vital for making key decisions at this early stage. Better to make the hard decisions now as it only gets more expensive in time and money the deeper into the project you go. It’s a hand-in-hand process with design and code, to check the viability of the new and cool ideas.


Beautiful corners


When the project is a little further along, you can undertake the beautiful corner process. A team of artists co-create a final room or level. Rather than building out a full 360-degree section of an environment, you build a small, dedicated space. This means that you only make what you need, not everything around you. It’s purely to cut down the time when making a section of the game look beautiful, including animations, VFX, materials and lighting.


Why do this? Well, ultimately you’re creating a visual benchmark to communicate to the team. To pass on ideas of mood, style, and composition of the game’s assets. Yes, you could build the full area if you wanted, and with game asset libraries available online, it’s getting easier and easier to achieve large-scale visual prototyping. But ultimately, it comes down to a balance of complexity and time. If sweeping changes are required, that’s a lot of work to be redone.


Research and development 


Right from the start of pre-production, you’ll be uncovering what’s possible for this game. You’ll have things you want to achieve and so will the designers. Working with your Tech Artists, graphics department and coders, you’ll be testing the limits of the hardware, finding out how much of your vision you can implement and where compromises are going to be had. 


Your Tech Artists work hand in hand with the graphics and game tech teams, and it’s their job to care about frame rates, overdraw, streaming, rigging, shaders or tools (there are more but it’s a long list!). Think of them as your favourite car mechanic, you know, the ones who can fix anything. 


Tech Artists support both the art team and engineers, giving them solutions to problems, helping to optimise assets, giving feedback on new systems and doling out advice where it’s most needed. Any new assets or crazy ideas the game needs should involve your tech team as it’s no use creating something that’s hugely inefficient, complex or too time and resource intensive. 


So, what happens next? 


Eventually, you’ll move out of pre-production into production, heading towards building the game’s vertical slice and then the final game. This is where things get really busy. It’s full steam ahead with your concept team and game artists. 


While in this period of heavy work, following the pipeline will help mitigate risk and reduce waste of artwork and levels. There’ll always be a few casualties of the creative process, but ideally you want to keep waste to a minimum. Sometimes it’s not in your control, as levels or assets can be altered or cut and there’s nothing you can do about it if the Creative Director says they have to go.


You’ll be in a constant cycle of reviewing at this point. As the games level and art assets progress through the art pipeline, you’ll be casting an eye over them at key points, checking in with your Leads, pushing ideas and tasks to your concept art team, marching at full pace towards the final game delivery and polish.


Anything else?


Probably yes, but I think it could be another book. Game development is a constant cycle of creation, review, and refinement. Eventually you turn that rough old rock into a diamond, and that’s how it is with the artwork too. All the aspects are developed in stages, advancing until they hit the final quality bar set by the visual targets.


Once you have assets, other departments can get involved, specifically, the cinematic and cut scene departments. On bigger titles, they’re their own department, with a Cinematic Director and whole duplicate art team.


Your game could be in development from two to six years, depending on the tech and the complexity. As with all artwork, it’s never really finished, it’s only ever just good enough and eventually you run out of time. The project, after many reviews and refinement cycles, nears the end and you enter the close-out phase of development.


Close-out (pre-release) 


This is the time most artists prefer not to think about. You’re approaching the end of the project and, while you might want it to slow down, the pressure starts to really ramp up. Some companies operate a culture of crunch, burning out their artists and teams to hit deadlines. Others, as you’ve read, actively try to avoid that, and keep morale and staff retention high.


Is it possible to avoid crunch? 


I’d like to think so, I’ve experienced both ends of the spectrum and I know which one I prefer! As an Art Director (AD), you think of your artists as your flock; you don’t want to put them through crunch and normally it’s not even your decision. It’s made at a higher level, when a company might say they are pulling the ‘crunch switch’ and define the minimum hours to be worked. I don’t think any artist is effective after weeks of fourteen-hour days, it’s a false economy. Let’s assume your team isn’t crunching, but they may complete a small amount of overtime, to get the polish in and make the product shine.


What’s happening in this period? 


As AD, you’ll be working with senior art staff and others to begin wrapping up. There’ll be a list of bugs as long as your arm to get fixed, including art polish tasks, collision errors or level-of-detail issues, streaming or missing textures, performance improving tasks, polygon reduction, draw call reduction, overdraw issues, character rigs going wacky, shadows flickering and polygon z depth issues, among many others. This is all part of the optimisation and bug-fixing pass.


You’ll be working with producers to evaluate the bugs, aiming to reach a point when the bug database is reducing, known as a burn rate. If the company hits the ideal rate, the chances for hitting the final deadlines are high. 


As QA test the game and enter bugs on the database, the bug count goes up. As developers fix them, the bug count slowly goes down, intending to fix more bugs than the number entered, aiming to hit zero. Each bug has a severity rating and a priority assigned to it before being sent to the Leads and then filtered out to the art team to fix. The closer to the final date you get, small bugs start to be ignored, then the medium ones, before only those assigned as critical are addressed.


It’s all about trying to keep the build stable, not wobbly like jelly, because the last thing you want is for the build to fall over and crash. Eventually each bug is reviewed carefully before being assigned to someone to fix. Ultimately your project’s Senior Producer will make the call and decide that whatever’s left in the bug database can be ignored; either because the visual or game play issue is negligent, or that it’s just too high risk. Often the art team will be ramping down, finishing their work ahead of you, but marketing and other things will still require your attention.


While the art team’s workload slows to a trickle, yours will continue with marketing requests, unless your company has a dedicated marketing team. Let’s peek at what’s in store, you might even like it!


Marketing (pre-release) 


I admit, I love making promo images. Just look at my portfolio, you’ll see it’s littered with them. 
www.pauldavidjones.com
 As an Art Director, if you don’t have a marketing department, you and a few others on the team will make and take images to fill the company’s needs. To accompany this, normally you’ll have a cinematics department tasked with creating some short sizzle videos to attract gamers.


In a gaming cycle, at various points in the year, publishers will be keen to show their wares. They want to bravely parade their next great title. Shows such as E3 have been a huge draw and companies often factor a demo into the schedule, so that they waste no work, and it continues to contribute to the overall development of the game. 


That’s not always the case though, and many working hours have been wasted in pursuit of the next great demo. Handled well, it can motivate the team to push that little bit harder, to hit the quality needed. Who doesn’t want to show something they are proud of? The downside of a badly planned demo is that it demoralises the team, and it takes longer to spool back up, and for the team to become fully effective again.


Demos are only one part of the marketing process, so let’s cover some promotional areas you might explore, depending on your company’s size and budget.


Media/Online articles 
– Magazine covers often require unfinished assets to be polished up and shoved in front of the camera. Because magazines take up so much space with their own name, image inserts, headlines and text, your base image can look weird on its own. It’s best to work with a template from either the magazine’s publisher or make your own from an existing cover found on the internet. Characters always have the greatest impact, followed by action and explosions. This is the truth! Also note that you may have to adopt different standards. In the past, I’ve had to create covers and not been allowed to do any Photoshop fix-ups. For others, they didn’t care if the image looked representative of the game or not.


E3 and trade show promo
 – Trade shows until recently have been part and parcel of game dev life, its a great opportunity for devs to meet up and for the public to get experience with their favourite titles in development. They often spend a small fortune on just dressing the stage, or using PCs with boosted specs which feature next-gen unreleased hardware. Of course, the visuals of the environment need to match. You’ll be involved in getting good information to model and set builders, working on posters and banners, and don’t forget the merchandise.


Game promo
 – Throughout the game’s development you’ll be called upon to provide some shots which show a slice of life or a sneak peek, something to whet the fans’ appetite. We sometimes term these ‘bullshots’ (not officially), because they are an imagined slice of life, so not in-game real-time action (random), but rather something that’s been carefully crafted or choreographed with everything turned up to the max. They are at the heart of what the developer wants for the game, but like everything in games, are subject to change.


Internal reviews
 – At key points in the year, you’re likely to have review sessions with the executive group, involving your studio’s upper management and the publishing group. Coffee and crepes, jugs of water and glasses all neatly arranged, that kind of thing. Large projects have gates to pass through for quality and direction control, so the execs want to see progress and know that it’s going in the direction they imagined. Part of this will involve presentations, and you’re likely to be involved with the content.


Box Art/Brochure/Manual/Strategy guides
 – If you’re working for a small-to-medium company, you might do the box art and supporting material. Alternatively, you might outsource it to a design agency, but either way, you’re going to be involved with direction. I made the LEGO box art with a small strike team and I’ve created game manuals, but I think that’s a thing of the past.


Website and social media
 – You might work for a small but well-funded start-up, in which case, they might want website banners made, or images collated to pass to the designer. If so, you’ll be an integral part of the company’s brand identity and website development.


Recruitment
 – Stunning images are required to entice candidates, including full-screen images or banners to show the art skills of the company, which is undoubtedly the quickest way to draw new talent’s attention. You might help recruitment with physical printed banners and management of the brand.


Corporate branding 
(including wall murals, business card and email signatures) – New offices will require a lick of paint or a complete refit. Your boss might want large wall murals or custom graphics that represent the game or company, with shiny new business cards and email signature banners. 


On completion of marketing requests, you might find a bit of breathing space, time to decompress and think about what’s next, which is good, you need a bit of time for your creative resources to regenerate. Take this downtime when and where you can, because, before you know it, you’ll be moving on to DLC (downloadable content).


Downloadable content (DLC – post-release) 


For a long time now, downloadable content (DLC) has been part of our lives. No longer do games have a clear beginning and end. DLC gives the player the option of lengthening their game-playing experience on their favourite game by paying for additional content.


As an Art Director, even before the dust has settled on the original game, you could find yourself starting to fire up the creative engine to work on new content. Often it’s new levels, characters, weapons, or vehicles. 


Depending on the type of game you work on, you could be in an endless cycle of creating additional content, especially for games that are considered ‘free to play’. Publishers recoup costs by providing loot boxes, character skins or upgrades; anything that can be monetised. 


Whatever your situation, I hope you’re more than a one-person army and have a trusted team of artists to work alongside you. My advice is always to look to spread the load; delegate where you can, push back when the pile is getting too big, and at some point, your manager must make a priority call. As the Art Director, you’ll always face a wall of work, but with experience, you’ll feel like you can handle the pressure and juggle the tasks which are part of everyday work.


At the end of development and on release, it’s always a great feeling of achievement when you see you and your teams creativity on store shelves, digitally or physically. 


The journey might have been difficult, the path may not have been straight and there definitely will have been some ups and downs, but I think it’s worth it in the end!













Level Five Summary 


Now you’ve read what’s in store, that puts you a step ahead of the rest. As you know though, knowing, doing and reacting are skills you’ll be honing right from the get-go, until things feel more natural. It’s a process of discovery, where situations and staff will test your resolve, but also one of great creative expansion, where you as an artist can push yourself and others in brave new directions. Your managerial skills will also grow, honed by leading people and providing direction with consistency and integrity. 


5.1 Overview


•
 Do you know what an Art Director (AD) really is now? 


•
 Some interesting reading from another AD, it’s a varied role.


•
 Did you find out more about yourself and the Art Director you want to be?


•
 Get your sales hat on, you’re about to become a showman, so channel your inner extrovert.


5.2 Management


•
 You will interact with many teams. Some of these you’ll direct, while others will take more collaboration and teamwork.


•
 Try different styles to find what works best for you and be able to alter things to suit the situation.


•
 Enjoy building your art team, because if you support and nurture them, your project will benefit.


•
 Making time for art is important so watch out for meetings taking over your day.


•
 Prepare for battle. Working with execs is never simple.


5.3 Art process


•
 Gather reference, gain inspiration, and share in a precise and deliberate way. There is no room for woolly thinking! 


•
 An art bible is a living document and will grow over the project’s development.


•
 Knowing how to work with your concept art team is pivotal to your success.


•
 Bringing in freelancers as extra help is great but understand the push and pull of art directing remotely.


•
 Your game art team will need support from you, including the Concept Artists and your Lead, so maintain regular reviews.


•
 If you need further firepower to get your game assets built on time, outsourcing is a potential option.


5.4 Putting it all together


•
 The start of a new project is exciting and challenging! Work with the team to put your stamp on it.


•
 One solution doesn’t fit all in discovering your new world using concept art, so be flexible on how you achieve it.


•
 Making games is a long process, so make sure you understand what’s required to avoid trouble later in your art pipeline.


•
 When a game enters its final stages, it’s time to make hard calls about what to fix and what to punt.


•
 Be proactive in the marketing of your game because the content that goes out to the public has real weight and sets expectations.


•
 Take advantage of any downtime, you’ll want to save some creative energy for DLC.


If you’re a Junior Artist reading this, I expect it could feel overwhelming. But don’t worry, you don’t have to think about this for a long, long time. At least you understand what’s going on behind the scenes and why you’ll see your Art Director less than you’d like!


If you are an artist ready to step into this role, I hope I’ve given you a good idea of the areas to tackle. All these skills require refinement and experimentation. You’ll hit some bumps along the way, maybe even a crash or two, but how you get up and recover is down to you. If you have a talented team around you, they will back you up as you support them, and this makes the process so much supportive. 


This is a top-tier role, and a challenging one, but it’s exciting and creative. Take your time, build on your strengths, develop your key team players and keep the gremlins at bay; before you know it, you’ll be making worlds everyone wants to be a part of. There’s nothing quite like that feeling, knowing you and your team have moved heaven and earth to create the best artwork you can, building something out of nothing – for us artists, can it get any better?













Achievements













A.1 New skills unlocked 


I hope you’ve enjoyed this book; you’ve taken quite the journey, and you’ve made it to the end! I feel like this part of the book should be animated, like a bunch of Mario Kart characters standing atop the podium, jumping about with ticker tape gently wafting down the screen, or some star shapes being filled in with solid gold, VFX exploding with each star’s completion. Whatever your preference, go ahead and fill those five stars right up – you deserve it!




So, what now? Bursting with all this new information and insider knowledge, what do you do?


If I were back at the start, I’d be grinning from ear to ear after reading this, I’d have an idea of my path, the hurdles I’m likely to face, the areas where, as an artist, I can improve and best of all, I know I need to improve my soft skills just as much as my creative ones!


As you climb the ranks within the industry, you can bring with you, like a tornado with a tractor and a cow (ever seen the movie, Twister?), a whole new level of educated artist, one who’s ready to take on the gaming world. You’ll be someone who can level their fellow artists up, to actively listen and not judge, to push boundaries and to bring everyone along with them. That’s the dream, right?


I can’t claim this book will make you an expert right away, you still have to do your part, learn your craft, improve your art skills, and push yourself to reach new heights on this crazy adventure. So, on that note, all that’s left for me to say is, climb game artist, climb; go plant that flag and make your mark in the games industry!

Glossary of terms 


There’s a lot of jargon that I take for granted in the industry, some of it, I even had to lookup myself to be sure I was using it correctly! This is my short and sweet version to help you along.


AAA
 – A company who makes a highly successful game on a large budget.


Art bible
 – A visual document, describing the vision and process to create artwork, normally built up in stages as the project progresses.


Beautiful corner
 – A Benchmark visual target, defining scope and quality for the art team to follow.


Draw calls
 – Instructions to the graphics processing unit (GPU) containing information about textures, shaders, buffer, vertices, etc. An ‘expensive asset’ traditionally is one with a lot of draw calls, and on its own is not a problem, but many different assets all with high draw call costs can reduce performance.


Draw distance
 – Affects the detail and how much is drawn by the graphics card and seen by the player. Long draw distances seen in many open world games require many optimisation tricks to recreate an object seen close vs far (see LOD).


FPS
 – Normally frames per second but can also be first person shooter, depending on context.


Game build
 – A running (or broken) version of the game. When new code or assets are added, new builds are triggered, giving the dev team a chance to review the alterations.


Game dev
 – Game developer, anyone on the team involved with directly making the game.


GDD
 – Game design document. A living document detailing the guiding vision used to develop the game, making it clear to everyone on the project how the game should be built and played.


LOD
 – Level of Detail. Over distance, assets swap out to be lower detail and less resource intensive, part of maintaining high frame rates. Usually by reducing triangle counts, materials used, animation bone numbers and rig complexity, turning of shadow casting, lowering particle count to name a few!


Modding
 – Someone who makes alterations to an existing product, ranging from improving textures to creating alternative player arenas for example or changing the rules of a game to create variety for players.


Overdraw
 – When a pixel is drawn more than once in a single frame. Excessive overdraw reduces performance and frame rate.


PBR
 – Physically Based Rendering. A rendering technique aiming to achieve high levels of realism, using textures and lighting to correctly describe an objects surface.


PDR
 – Personal Development Review. A meeting in which a manager discusses with their artists individually their previous month’s, quarter’s, half-year’s or year’s performance.


Pipeline
 – The process of building a game from start to finish. Each department will also have their own pipelines, which ultimately merge to create assets, levels and gameplay.


Poly
 – Short for Polygon. 3D models are built using polygons, normally three sided (‘Tri’) or  four sided (Quad) for best results. 


QA
 – Quality assurance, also known as testers. This department is responsible for finding bugs in the game while it’s in development. A cycle of identifying, reproducing, and reporting.


Stand-up 
– Part of the Agile methodology. It’s a short meeting where the team talks while standing. Each team member talks about important tasks that are about to be started, in progress or about to be finished. 


Target frame rate
 – Desired rate of new images (frames) to be shown, to effectively give the impression of movement. Games like to target 30 or 60 FPS (frames per second). Low frame rates give a poor experience when playing the game.


Tech Art
 – Known as a Technical Artist if you work in this department. Extremely varied in its remit but basically, Tech Art provides solutions to integrating art assets into the engine, setting and hitting technical limits while aiming to keep the art still high quality.


Wacom Pen
 – Wacom is a company that makes digital drawing tablets, the pen, does the drawing.  There are other makes (cheaper and arguably just as good) such as XP-Pen.













Acknowledgements


Writing a book is one of the most insane projects I’ve undertaken. To anyone who has written and published, I salute you.


Through thick and thin, my good days and bad, mini meltdowns and poor sleep, my wife has remained my best friend and my rock. Mickela’s sage advice of ‘for your first draft, just write, even if you think it’s awful, keep on writing, don’t stop,’ kept me going. Without it, this book would have crashed on the rocks of doubt and I’d always regret never getting my word out.


I am deeply thankful to my children. Olivia, who gave me the boost I needed, helping write my initial schedule on a scrappy piece of paper and pushing me towards my first draft goal. Sebbie, who during Covid shared my study and became my crypto trading buddy (we both lost our fortunes – ahem, well $200), but he gave me advice on book cover ideas from multiple concepts I made.


I could not have reached the end of my book journey without the gentle but steady hand of my editor, Jasmin, who accepted the challenge and helped me shape the book into what it is today.


A big shout out to the self-publishing formula community, the weekly videos were a regular point of inspiration and learning. I’ve learnt so much, and I still have far to go!


Finally, a nod and smile to everyone I’ve worked with in the games industry. You know who you are; you know it’s demanding work and you know, at the end, it’s all worthwhile.
